On the treatment of plagues.
I have already mentioned in the section on the treatment of plagues what is suitable for the book, and I will now describe the treatment of those with plague using medicines according to the regimen.
Therefore, I say that plagues are diverse depending on what causes the plagues and depending on the location where the plague occurs.
And the causes for various types of plagues are as follows: like a stone thrown, or a cut by a sword or knife, or a blow with a lance, or a stick, or an arrow, or similar to those from various causes.
Here are the plagues that are in the parts of the body such as the plague which affects the head, the neck, the feet, the belly, the groin, and similar ones to those affecting the limbs.
And I also recommend the treatment of certain plagues, so that you pause that reasoning and rule regarding other plagues.
And I will begin with the simple plagues of the head, for the recall of the compound ones will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore, I say that when you encounter a simple plague in the head and it is not a fracture, then consider: if it is due to an impact of a stone or similar object and has merely bruised the bone but is significant enough for you to fear that an abscess might form, at this time, you should immediately apply leeches or perform phlebotomy on the cephalic veins without delay; do not postpone this if there is no prohibition against phlebotomy based on some other cause, especially if little blood flows from the wound of the plague.
And the phlebotomy should be performed on the opposite side of the affected area; and ensure that the patient is guarded against overeating and heavy foods.
But if some cause prohibits phlebotomy in detail, then reduce the patient's food intake and do not allow meat or strong foods.
And pause treatment on the plague if an abscess forms, and then apply it only in rose oil or in wine that has been stypticized.
But if you are sure of an abscess forming, then pause treatment on the plague if it is recent and the patient's blood and air have not changed; in that case, apply the powder I recommend sparingly.
And if the air has already changed that, then pause for some time until it is healed.
And if the wound is large or due to a division of tissue and similar cases, and if it cannot be closed with stitches using needles, then close those with sutures as I will explain regarding the suture of them.

And if you find that the stitch has become putrid and does not adhere to the bone, then incise all of that rotten part and remove it, and immediately treat it.

And the needles should be of medium fineness between coarseness and subtlety, for fine needles pierce the flesh quickly; but thick ones are difficult to insert into the tissue, and therefore it is best that they be of medium fineness and thickness.
And it is not advisable to place the needle at both extremes of the wound in a straight line, as this may cause the flesh to be pierced too quickly and the wound to separate before it has properly healed.
And do not extend the stitches too much so that the wound is not hindered from healing properly.
And then cut the ends of the needles so that they do not cause irritation to the patient while sleeping; and place over the sutures small padded cushions from all sides, which should cover the ends of the needles; and leave them in place until you know that the wound has healed properly.
And this type of suture with needles is most convenient for small wounds, as you only need one or two needles or around that number.
But the second type of suture is common: it involves suturing all four corners, two from the wound and two from the circumference, at once, with a needle that has been properly waxed in terms of fineness and thickness.

On the treatment of abscesses and fistulas.
Know that every wound and abscess when it becomes old and chronic, and forms an ulcer that is not consolidated and continues to discharge over time without being excised, whether in any limb, is universally called a fistula, and we refer to this as an abscess.
And truly, a fistula is a felted nodulation, hard and white, at which there is no pain but rather a sensation like the itching of bird’s pinions; and for this reason it is called a bothersome itch, that is, an irritation.
And it is in some people a moist fistula, which discharges fluid; and in some cases the moisture can be excised within a few hours. In some cases, this humidity is moved, and it is little, and it is thick, and it is subtle.
I have already shown all those causes, the signs of them, and their cure with medicines in the division.
And it is necessary for me to tell you about the treatment of fistulas, which is done through surgical operation.
And you should know that fistulas occur in all parts of the body; among them are those that extend to large veins and arteries, or to nerves, or to the brain, or to the intestine, or to the bladder, or to some of the ribs, or to some of the vertebrae of the back, or to some of the compound joints such as that of the hand and foot; and there are those that affect the vicinity of major limbs and similar areas.
And therefore, among these fistulas that follow this method, they are diseases difficult to heal, and do not easily receive a cure, and dealing with them requires effort and ignorance.
And if any of the fistulas has a depth which does not reach to those places that were mentioned, and you hope for and trust in its cure, and wish to gain knowledge of it, then take a probe of iron or steel, and if the fistula goes along straight, explore it with this.
And if the fistula is tortuous, then investigate it with a probe of thin lead, for the lightness of its body allows it to glide easily along the curvature.
And if the fistula has openings that are horrible to see, it is not within your power to signify upon them with a probe.
Then irrigate the openings through those openings, for the moisture with which you irrigate that one will go to the other openings and flow from them.
And then inquire meticulously to determine if there is bone or nerve, and whether the depth of the fistula is distant or near. Also consider whether it is a fistula for which all its openings are evident at once within your full capacity, within your thorough investigation, with which you investigate the malady, and the cause of the pain under your compression on the spot, and similar signs and accidental causes related to the abscess.
And when you have established all those causes with true judgment, then proceed to the treatment with confidence: and this is that you consider now whether it is a fistula that is apparent and close, or in a location near a joint, or nerve, artery, vein, or one of the places I have told you about.
Now you should treat the fistula as described in its narration, and remove what is within that filtration and of corrupted flesh, and what has grown out from the opening of the flesh or tissue, and care for it until it is healed.
If the fistula is of great depth and follows a straight course, it becomes necessary to incise it as far inwards as you can, and then remove all the corrupted flesh; afterwards apply plasters wrapped in sharp medicines, and insert them into the cavity of the fistula which you have probed with iron.
And do this several times so that the sharp medicine may dissolve all that is rotten in the cavity of the fistula from corruption.
And then treat it with applications that generate healthy flesh until it is healed.
But if it does not heal from this treatment, then you should cauterize it as we have recommended.
But if the cause of the fistula is bone and you need to determine this, then you should probe it and dry it as we have recommended, unless you prohibit any involvement with veins or nerves or a major limb as we have said.

On the treatment of hemorrhoids and the quality of enemas with medicines.

Regarding the treatment of nasal ailments.
This illness is called "nafir" in our land, and it is a pain that occurs in certain limbs and then changes from one limb to another.
And I have already seen that as I will now describe to you. I was called to attend to a sick woman in a village, and when I uncovered her arm, I saw a small inflammation in the vein called funis on the arm. After it had been punctured for about an hour, I saw that inflammation moving, like a worm climbing up this way, but more quickly than one would expect, as if it were living silver moving from place to place.
Then let the pain move from that place and settle in the muscle, and stay here for about one hour.
And then move to the other side until it reaches the other arm.
And then I returned to the middle, where it was rotating throughout the body as I saw fit; and I was very amazed by the permutation of it and the speed at which it moved from member to member. And I had already seen this before in my eyes according to that method, but now I have witnessed the pain being transferred from one member to another. But I did not see this in my eyes as I did in the woman.
And I did not think this could be so unless it was because the woman was of such delicate constitution, with her two bodies and exposed veins, and in this way the airiness is perceived to be transferred.
And it is necessary that this does not appear according to this retroversion in those who have delicate and moist bodies with prominent veins.
And when you want to treat it, and the patient feels that pain, then do not apply it to the eye as we have said, but instead press firmly on that area until the trapped air is released, and cauterize the location.

On the extraction of arrows.
Arrows are diverse in kind and vary according to the parts of the body into which they strike. Their diversity is such that some have large points, others small points, some concave, others blunt; and among them there are those that are solid. Some arrows have three edges, while others have tongues or barbs.
And the signs of an arrow striking the head are when the arrow penetrates and passes through the bone and emerges from the cephalus, which is above the brain. This occurs with sudden violent pain, scotoma (partial loss of vision), vertigo, stiffness in the eyes, inflammation and redness of the tongue, spasms, a mixture of understanding (confusion or altered perception), vomiting of bile, and speech may become impaired, and from the wound site comes out white moist substance similar to dust, and the appearance suggests an excess of moisture.
And if these signs appear to you, then refrain from treatment and the extraction of the arrow unless it is outside the head.
Here are the signs when an arrow hits the heart: it is near the left nipple, and you can feel that it is embedded in a hard object rather than in soft tissue; and the movement of the arrow is like the movement of a pulse, and there comes black blood from the wound, and coldness spreads to the extremities, and cold sweat appears, and fainting occurs; therefore, you know that death will come very soon.
And the signs when the arrow has pierced the lung are frothy blood from the wound, and the veins near the neck become swollen, and the complexion of the person changes, and they exhale a large breath, and they crave to breathe in air deeply.
And if the arrow hits the membrane that is in the chest, and the pain at that moment is near the small ribs, and it causes great pain and sighing, and there is no movement of any of the body's muscles.
And if the arrow hits the stomach, immediately there will be vomit of some undigested food.
And if the arrow hits the abdomen and is embedded there, causing a wound to split open, or damaging the membrane or bowel which is ruptured, then there is no hope for recovery from the extraction of the arrow.
And if the arrow hits the bladder and urine comes out from it, and there is leakage to other parts outside, and immediately there is pain over the patient, then you know that it is fatal.
And other limbs as well, such as the face, the neck, and the jaw, the shoulders and the arm muscles, the spine of the back, the collarbone, the chest, the ribs, and similar parts of the limbs, are saved according to their major functions when the arrow does not hit a vein or artery and is not a poisonous arrow.

It was a large sharp Turkish arrow with an iron tip shaped like a lion’s head, which had no feathers.
And the Jew healed, and no harm came to his eye.

There is also the funis of the arm, which lies over the elbow, and this is where phlebotomy is performed; it appears prominently on the forearm. Lastly, there is the salvatella vein, located between the thumb and the little finger, appearing in two branches.

And when it appears, then perform phlebotomy on that area according to each individual case as is possible and according to the transverse position, which is better; or incise that area by incision or cut it by cutting, since the location is bony.
And it is in the words "halfway between"; and if you find that area, and it does not appear to full sensation, then perform phlebotomy on one of its branches, which appears on the foot between the thumb and the ring finger.
And be careful of the nerves, and let the blood flow in the quantity that you desire.
Then loosen the ligature and apply pressure over the site of the phlebotomy cut and strengthen the area, for it heals quickly.
On wind exposure and the qualities of using those [methods].
Wind injuries are mostly to horns, or ears, or eyes.
Wind exposure is done in two ways: one involves incision and bloodletting, and the other is wind exposure without incision.
And this wind exposure that is without incision is done in two ways, either with fire or without fire.
And for wind exposures that are administered with incision and bloodletting, there are fourteen sites on the body, of which one is where wind exposure for alnocrati purposes is applied, at the back part of the head; another site is near both sides of the neck; wind exposure of the chin below the jaw, next to the mouth; wind exposure of both cheekbones; wind exposure of alohoster, which is above the temples; wind exposure of two armpits; wind exposure of both upper arms; and wind exposure of both thighs.
Wind exposure does not extract blood but from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and through this, one does not capture the virtue as is done with phlebotomy; nor is it necessary for wind exposure to be performed with or without fire in any disease caused by internal congestion until the entire body is evacuated.
But if necessity prompts wind exposure due to illness or for a continuous period, administer it at any time at the beginning of the month and at the end of it, in the middle of the month and at some times during it.
And when you encounter any of those causes, and particularly if it is in the third or middle part of the month, I command that bleeding be performed two or three hours after sunset.
And the benefit of bleeding from the neck is that when you encounter heaviness in the head and sinking to the eyes; but it should be done near complete evacuation of the entire body.
And this bleeding is in the site of phlebotomy of the cephalic region.
And it is not appropriate to use bleeding on one who is completely exhausted, or in one who has a cold, for it causes great harm to them.
And because of this, it is not appropriate to use that on the elderly, and in those whose heads are often ill.
But for one who is repeatedly bled in this manner, memory loss may occur; and because of this, you should command the person performing the bleeding to pause their hand briefly after removing the cup to prevent the generation of forgetfulness.

Thus begins the chapter of the third part: on restoration. This chapter is considered necessary in the art of medicine; it is the restoration of fractures and dislocations of bones.
Oh, son, be aware that this chapter is boasted about by foolish doctors and the vulgar, and by those who for any length of time have not heeded the books of the Ancients nor read a single letter from them.
And for this reason it is done that this method of the scientists in our land is private; and I have not forgotten any of it in all that time; and I have not applied what follows, but through the length of reading the books of the Ancients and studying to understand them, extracting the science from them.
And subsequently I directed myself to experimentation and study throughout the duration of my life.
And I have already described in this chapter everything that my science and experience contain; now I condense it for you to spare you the burden of lengthiness; I abbreviate it at the end of brevity; and demonstrate it at the end of clarity; and present many forms of instruments used in those treatments, as is done through addition of explanation, just as I have done in two preceding chapters.
First chapter.
A summary and collection of what is necessary for the treatment of fractures of bones, including those prerequisites that are essential before we begin to recall the parts of broken limbs and their various treatments. Therefore, let us begin the prologue of this chapter with a summary of the discourse; and the chapters will be presented to those whose understanding requires that they first pause on the truth of these matters; so you and others like you who wish to know or learn this noble art from you.
And therefore I say that when it happens to someone to suffer a fracture, dislocation, torsion, or fall, at that moment it is necessary for you to consider at the beginning whether to perform venesection or to wait, depending on the circumstances, unless some cause prohibits it, such as weakness of strength, or if the person who suffers from it is an infant, elderly and decrepit, or if it is a time of extreme heat or severe cold.
And let the patient be content with light food such as fresh herbs and meat of birds and small animals; we prohibit heavy meats and foods that fill the body, and all kinds of food that fill the veins with blood, until you are sure of a safe condition regarding abscesses, and if you do not fear an effusion of matter into the limb.
And now return the patient to their usual regimen that they were accustomed to follow.
And when the broken bone begins to be restored, at this time it is necessary for the patient to be fed with nutritious foods that are moist and heavy, which have viscosity, such as foods made from boiled wheat with water, rice, peas, beans, cow's stomachs, hens, fresh cheese, thick soups, and similar items. With this regimen, coagulation of the fracture occurs more easily and better.
And know that the bones in those who are thin, just as in infants and in the elderly, cannot be joined and consolidated according to their nature by the drying of the limbs.
However, nature adjusts and applies them over the broken bone from every part for some cause similar to glue, in which there is a thickness that adheres and strengthens it as they are conjoined together, until they come to an end of strength and firmness, just as fire at the beginning, without any cause prohibiting them from completing their work.
And for this reason it is necessary that the patient abstain from foods that are heavy, viscous, and thick, as we have said.
And know that the types of fractures are diverse according to the diversity of the limbs, for a fracture of the bone in the arm is different from a fracture of the bone in the head; and a fracture of the bone in the foot is different from a fracture of the bone in the back; and similarly all other members are different among themselves.
And I wish to provide a recapitulation of every type of fracture, explained in its own separate chapter.
The diversity of fractures within themselves. And the types of fractures in bones are diverse in their locations; for one kind of curved fracture is different from that which occurs in the stars [likely meaning "skull"]; and some transverse fractures occur according to the length of the bone: and among the fractures of the shaft, splinters, and comminuted injuries, both those that proceed and do not proceed, just as when paper is prepared for writing, there is a fracture with a crack and disruption in the head, and it is a small fissure fracture.
And for each type there is a particular torment proper in the restoration of the second, which will come as a recall of the distinct ones in their places.
A sign of a bone fracture. And by this a bone fracture is known: its twisting, and the prominence and appearance of it to the touch, and how it feels when you press on it with your hand.
And therefore when there is no visible twisting or deformation and when you touch the bone with movement and the patient does not feel great pain, then there is no fracture here.
There might be twisting or a small fissure, so do not press on it with your hand and through compression of the entire area; instead, apply one of the medicines about which there will be a recall at that moment when you consider appropriate; and it may be tight due to subtle constriction.
And you should know that the place where it is curved and not split into two or more as occurs in the shaft, as the whole part of its comparison is bent, then you should begin from your time to the correction of what affects it until an abscess forms.
And if an abscess occurs in it, do not delay for any number of days waiting for the abscess to cease; rather, bind that according to every method you can by lightness and ingenuity.
And know that the restoration of it and the alignment of it is quicker than that of the bone in which such dislocation occurs.
And then strengthen that according to what will come to your recollection of it.
But if the bone is dislocated, then it is necessary to extend this broken limb from two parts, whether it be the hand or the foot; or by your hand if the member is small; or by two ropes or by one rope and your hand.
And place yourself in a position where you can put the limb back in its proper location according to its natural shape, while the parts of the broken member are extended, then force yourself to return those adductions with skill and according to every method that you can into their places.
And guard yourself according to your strength so that no harm befalls the patient due to your operation or injury; and force yourself according to your ability to apply one bone to its companion bone according to their best forms.
And it is necessary that at that opportune moment you act skillfully with your hand, and if you see any divergent cause, correct that and adjust it according to the extent of your ability.
And avoid excessive extension and compression, just as some foolish doctors often cause an abscess by their operation, or cause contraction in the limb as we have seen happen due to their repeated handling.
And the quality of the bandaging of the tranquilized limb is what I recommend; for you should know that broken limbs vary in their smallness and largeness and in the form of each one.
And for those that are small like the arm and the hand and the forearm and similar to these, then the wrapping should be of soft fine cloth.
But for those that are large like the thigh or the chest, it is necessary to wrap them with linen and a hard material, because the wrapping of linen connects the large limb and strengthens it evenly in all directions, so that no dirt enters.
And when you begin, after proper alignment of the fracture, pause conveniently for that, at the site of the fracture with three or four wrappings depending on the limb; and tighten your hand slightly with the bandage.
And then proceed to the upper part of the site of the fracture, and tighten moderately so that your bandaging does not hurt at the place where you have already tightened the fracture. From around the fracture, extend the bandage a little, and gradually loosen the tightness, until you take from the area a large part.
Then take another bandage and wrap it around the fractured area one more time, and then tie the bandages to a part further down from the fracture; and your operation should be in the tightening of the bandage and the loosening of that as we have said about the first bandage above.
Then place between the bandages a piece of linen or cloth that matches the curvature of the fracture if there is any curvature; and if not, do not pause for any reason in that area.
Then place another bandage on top, and immediately apply plasters properly made if there is no inflammation or abscess in the area, and at that time place over what causes the abscess and reduces the inflammation; and leave it like this for several days; and then you will strengthen it with the plasters.
And let the plasters be about half a palm wide and well made; or they can be of willow bark; or of palm leaves; and the width of each plaster should be according to this form and figure:
However, the plaster that is placed over the fracture should be slightly thicker and wider than the others, and the length of the plaster should match the size of the limb; then reinforce the plasters with another bandage according to your initial tightening; and tie it above with a knot properly as we have said about tightening, and this should be your tightening more so near the location of the fracture, and the further away from the fracture, the less should be the tightening.
And let the threads be of medium thickness and fineness, and let them be soft; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen in some foolish surgeons using cords from a sheep's gut for their stitches, it is a great error, when one sees the tension uneven.
And likewise, fine threads are not suitable either, for you will not achieve the desired tension with them, and it is necessary that there be at least a finger's width between stitch and stitch.
And if the patient is in pain at the ends of the threads near the stitches in those places, then pause and use threads of linen or carded wool until it does not cause injury to them.
Sometimes fractures occur due to a blow, and there is a disruption in the bone, and I will recall this case in the chapter dedicated specifically to such singularities.
And know that not all broken limbs should be set with stitches on the first day; the reason for this is that when the limb is large, it should not be placed in bandages but left loose for about five or seven days, or more, depending on your certainty regarding the risk of suppuration.
And this is the method of plasters used by the Ancients for treatment, which were applied over fractures, dislocations, and contortions.
Method of applying a plaster for the quick restoration of a fracture, suitable for various injuries and particularly for women and children, as it does not cause heat or irritation: take powdered flour, which is the fine residue that adheres to the walls of the mill during grinding.
And confine it as it is, but sieve that with white cloth, and pause the preparation if it is not thin or subtle; and then use that.
Another method for plasters to treat fractures, dislocations, and twists: recipe for asafetida, ledanum, acacia, inula, and migraech, which is the seed of wild millet, each one drachm; of myrrh and aloes each five drachms; aloe juice twenty drachms; all should be ground and sieved together with aqua alichil or with white cloth if the preparation of the plaster is heated; and then use this plaster, for it is well composed and quickly restores broken bones, and confers great benefit to many people.
Another method for a plaster to treat fractures and twists: recipe for migraech (seed of wild millet) and juice of white mulberry each one drachm; myrrh and aloes each five drachms; acacia six drachms; Armenian bole twenty drachms.
All should be triturated in a mortar with a stone pestle, and then sieved, and mixed with water or white cloth if the preparation is heated, and it should be administered.
Method for a plaster for joints and bones that have moved from their places, and for severe pain in those areas and for cracks and fractures: take clean wool and immerse it in sour wine and hot oil and apply it to the area.
Moreover, in this plaster there is no virtue of restoration, but it is good for treating boils and properly expelling pains.
Another method for a plaster that restores a broken bone: take fresh mulberry leaves and wild poppy leaves; mix them together and make a plaster from them.
Method of making a quick plaster to administer for the restoration of a bone fracture and when you want resolution of the remaining abscess: take rue, camomile flowers, violets, and flour of barley, of each one part; mix all together and cook with wine, but do not scald the member.
If it does scald, now confect that with cold cilantro water or with water and use it.
Another method of making a stronger plaster for resolution than this first one, when there occurs a hard abscess during the restoration of the limb: take rue, linseed, hawthorn, melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile, of each one equal part; mix all together and cook with sage water or with sweet water or with wine, whichever suits the quality of the limb and according to the condition of its color.
However, Hippocrates does not say in his book that it should be paused on the broken limb during its restoration except for a cerote made of wax and oil, and nothing else; he recommends that it should be midway between coarseness and fineness.
However, Galen is seen to have applied them on the broken limb during its restoration and for causes where there is slight drying with warmth as well as substances like myrrh and onions, similar to those causes which we have mentioned.
Method of making a plaster to relieve injury and pain: take linseed, chickpeas, human hair or bird's feathers, rue, and salt, of each equal parts; mix them together, sift them, and cook them, then make the plaster.
The quantity according to which it is necessary for the bandage to remain and then be loosened is that you consider whether pain does not occur or improvement does not happen and the bone is not dislocated from its place; then you do not loosen it for several days. And if intense pain or improvement or inflammation occurs at the site, then you should be cautious or loosen that bandage immediately, and remove the plaster.
And take a cloth and sponge made of marine leather and soak it in cold water, then with that wash the site until the inflammation subsides and the pain is alleviated; then allow the limb to rest for an hour; afterwards place it on wool soaked in sour wine and oil or scented oil, and bandage it for one night until you are sure of no abscess forming, and until the swelling of the limb subsides and the pain is reduced.
And then iteratively apply that gentle bandaging and small plaster without tightening it as much as your initial constriction, and gently do this until it is healed.
And if you find that the abscess and hardness and pain and swelling and all other symptoms have subsided completely, and you need a plaster and bandaging, then proceed as you did at the beginning similarly.
And if nothing happens to the limb from any of the causes we have mentioned, then do not loosen it until after three days or four or ten or seven; and in some cases all this is left for twenty days, as you see fit by loosening the bandage, as we have said, until the fracture adheres, and the coagulation of the flesh around it sets.
At this point, adjust the tightness more than in your first initial tightening, and adjust according to the swelling of the patient's body, as was previously mentioned.
If you see the site of the fracture already drying and withering more than it should, then you will know that nutrition is prohibited from reaching there; therefore, apply tepid water compresses to that area every day for three days; and loosen the bandage slightly, so this operation directs nutrition to the limb, and it heals quickly.
However, what the inexperienced restorers do when restoring a fractured limb later than it should be and in a tortuous manner, according to their procedure, is an error and causes great fear; if this were correct, the Ancients would have enlarged upon it in their books and approved such practice.
And I have not seen any of them do that, which is why it is more reasonable to not do so.
Chapter Second: Concerning fractures when they occur in the head.
The types of fractures are many, and their figures vary, and the causes are distinct. Some fractures are caused by the impact of an object, which removes all the bone within the cranium, leaving behind only the inner layer of bone, similar to what happens in wood when struck; thus, this type is called a concussion. Or an object may cut some part of the bone, cutting through the flesh but not penetrating further inside; and this type of fracture is called absolute elevation.
And the severity of these two fractures can be either great or slight, and there is also the fracture known as comminuted or crushed; the cause of this is impact from a weak object or falling on a stone, and similar circumstances.
And this fracture is penetrating which is already approaching the panniculus, which is below the bone; or it is on the face of the bone, and the severity of these two fractures is great or little.
And of the fracture which is weakened in the bone, in the subtlety of the thin strands, and by this it is named this species of fracture capillar.
And it is a fracture that is made either by falling or by the impact of a stone and similar objects, which causes some lamina of the bone to enter into the soft parts inside, and it is made in the concave place as well as when it happens to rib bones when they are impacted and split upon entering the inner parts.
And this is mostly made in the bones of human bodies, as well as such heads of children.
And for all these kinds of fractures, treatments are used, which are splints or bandages separated from each other. And I will return to the reminder of all this in its place.
And you will know all these kinds of fractures by detection on them and by inquiry into their weaknesses and the appearance of corrupted flesh that is upon them.
And the capillary fracture is known by this: it is revealed on the bone, and when it is wiped clean, it appears as a weakness on it, because the fracture appears black.
But in the treatment of fractures, consider at the beginning the symptoms of the patient, and if you see from these symptoms that it signifies by clear indication fear, as with vomiting of bile, extension, loss of understanding, hoarseness, fainting, acute fever, and similar symptoms of distress and rigidity, and such are present in the major conditions, then doubt whether they will be healed.
And if for unusual accidents you hope for a cure, then at that time you begin the treatment of them.
Which cause is four when the patient comes to you at the beginning as a patient and this is in the days of winter, then it is necessary that you study the condition of the bone until the day fourteen according to all disposition.
And if it is in the days of summer, then it is necessary that you wait to remove the bone until the seventh day, because what is before the bone may become gangrenous, and accidents which we have recounted may occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the membrane covering the brain and is divided into small parts and is torn, then it is necessary that you excise the comminuted and torn part, as I shall recount to you.
And it is that you shave the head of the patient, and expose the bone according to custom and method as best you can according to the figure of the injury, so that it is clear on the patient.
And if it happens to you that you need the detection of the bone, bleeding or an abscess cauterized, then you must proceed with what is necessary, which is to apply the area with cloths infused in vinegar and rose water, and let the wound rest until the abscess is subdued, and be sure there is no more bleeding; and afterwards begin the circuit around the bone and its removal.
And this should be done according to one of two methods of operation.
And one method is that you excise the bone with narrow forceps, and this is the shape of it: And then apply another excisor slightly wider than the first; and this is the shape of it:
Then administer myrrh to cleanse the pus that remains.
And it is necessary for you to have various scalpels, some of which are used for discharging pus more effectively than others; and some are sharper at their tips than the others, and they should be made of iron or steel.
And use them with ease in tapping on the scalpels so that you do not injure the head and the patient.
And if the bone is hard and strong, it is necessary to drill around it before you use the scalpels, which are called non-penetrating forceps.
And they are called this because these forceps do not reach the bone of the skull to the point where it is adjacent to the head, as their tip is rounded or has a margin above it resembling a small border or circle, which prevents them from penetrating beyond the thickness of the bone.
It is convenient for you to have many of these forceps, each of which should be suitable for the thickness of different bones, so that at your disposal there are various types covering the entire skull, with one end being sharp and the length and width varying according to the thickness of the bone.
This is the method for three types of forceps: The quality of perforating around a broken bone is that you place the forceps on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated; then move the forceps to another location, and continue this process throughout the extent of the thickness of the shaft or similar areas.
From the incision of the knife that is between each tract of the bone, and apply that poultice as finely as you can according to its lightness, as we have said, until you remove the bone, or with your hand, or by any other cause through some instruments which you have prepared for this, such as towels and fine forceps.
And you must ensure with all caution that the knife does not touch any part of the wound.
And when you have circled the perforated bone, it will be separated from the wound if it is continuous at that point, and you will extract it; you must rasp and smooth that in the roughness of where it is in the bone with an instrument similar to a knife, but it must be thinner and finer than other knives.
And if any part of the small and fragile bones breaks, now take that with ease according to what you need from the instruments; and then treat the wound with lint and ointments which we have mentioned.
And the other method of operation is light, distant from fear, which Guillaume and the laureate praise greatly; and this is the speech about it: It is necessary for you at the beginning to start uncovering the part of the bone in the place where the fracture is most severe and obvious, until when you have uncovered that part and you have paused below it with the extremity of this slow and cheerful knife, whose form is as follows:
And it is that the lens-shaped part is firm and no part is cut, and the sharp part is made into two flaps forward along its length, as you see; that the lens-shaped part be fitted to the wound with the incision side sharp in the bone.
Here is the translation:
In such cases if there is any cause attached to the bone of the abdomen in some places of bone, cut with that part of the scalpel which has a lens-shaped edge, and deliver what can be delivered easily, for it is delivered without harm or fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the abdomen, and that which is abscised or remains of the bone is its surface, and it remains in roughness and small projections, then you should scrape off that roughness and eradicate those projections with fine razors which you have prepared in various quantities so that it may be possible for you to administer those most suitable places and most appropriately according to where your operation on the broken bone leads you and its shape.
And you should administer in your proportion with which you scrape the bone at the beginning with the broadest of those razors, and gradually use one that is finer and do not cease doing this continuously until you reach administering the thinnest and finest of all of them.
But in each of the other small, fine fractures and subtle fractures, you should administer care according to what leads you towards the correction of them.
And it is because they are not afraid about all those who have studied in that art. And stand on what we have written regarding large fractures.
And if the abdomen is swollen when you eradicate the bone, then take a piece of cloth according to the size of the wound, and immerse it in boiled oil, and place that on the wound's opening; and then take another folded or triple-layered cloth and immerse it in vinegar and boiled oil, and place that over the first cloth; and do this as lightly as possible so as not to press down hard on the swelling; and then apply a broad bandage over it, but do not tighten it beyond what is necessary for the wound.
If you are certain that this cure should be applied for one day or two before you are sure of an abscess being cauterized; and then untie it and promptly administer medicines with which excessive dryness is superfluous, such as lint and flour of oats, and powder of iris and artemisia, and similar to those.
It is that you should make these medicines into powder which should be finely ground like those that are dry; and generally, administer everything to it whose property is to cleanse without irritating.
And guard according to your virtue that the plague in your cure be clean; and let it not be in the ignorance of handling or otherwise; nor should you allow its virulence to increase. For when it is adjusted upon the pannicle of the brain, it corrupts that and ruins it, and the malady will quickly turn malignant thereafter.
And it affects the top of the head, as the bone is cleansed there and especially when the cure is improperly applied, and a blackness appears on its surface; and therefore consider if the malady presents the symptoms we have mentioned, know that it is dangerous without any doubt.
And if it is not blackness but the medicine that is applied above which causes it, then you should take one part of honey and three parts of olive oil, and use them to moisten a piece of linen, and then apply that to the top of the head; and promptly cure it with appropriate medicinal spices until it is healed.
Chapter III.
On the restoration of the nose when it is cut.
Know that the nose is not broken except in two main places, or one of several possible areas, because there are two bones there, since the lower part is cartilaginous, and it is not broken, but only bruised, swollen, and disfigured.
If one of the two main parts is broken, then you should insert your little finger into the nasal cavity, and manipulate that fracture from within with your index finger, and from without with your thumb, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And do this with ease, and be careful that your operation does not cause the patient pain.
And if the fracture is in the uppermost part of the nose, and your finger cannot reach it, then you should equalize at the end of the bone where there is a small swelling.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you should proceed as described.
And try to restore it on the first day of the fracture if possible; otherwise, wait until the seventh or tenth day when the swelling has subsided.
And after that, insert at the end of the nose a pad of linen cloth if the fracture is in one place, or use two pads if the fracture is in two places, and let the pad be thick enough to fill the cavity of the nose.
And some of the ancient restorers say that the pads should be soaked in honey and changed every day, but I have not seen this necessary; instead, it is best to soak the pad in albumen of a cooked egg mixed with powder; and then leave the pad in place until the bone is firm and the cartilage has hardened.
In some cases, insert into the nose, instead of a sponge, a piece of cloth made into a ball wrapped in lion's skin; and let it be that with such force has the fracture of the nose occurred that the patient is not prohibited from breathing.
And this cause is not necessary for you; and if you want, do that, and if you want, use sponges.
And if another swelling occurs that needs to be cauterized, apply a plaster to the nose with pitch or wax mixed with vinegar and rose oil, or with aqua regia.
But if no swelling occurs that needs to be cauterized, now you should apply a plaster over it with sesame flour and powdered eggshell mixed with egg white; and then place it on the lion's skin, and do not cover the nose with any other cause.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or comminuted, now you should cut around them, and extract them with instruments that are suitable for this; and then close the wound, and treat it with substances that promote healing and consolidation, and with plasters appropriate for this.
And if you encounter an internal swelling of the nose, now you should treat it with sponges and administer lead tubes inside until it is completely healed.
Chapter IV.
On the restoration of the jaw when it is dislocated.
When the lower jaw is broken and there is no swelling around the fracture, now observe if the fracture is only on the outside, and it is not broken into two parts and curved inward, then its diagnosis is easy.
If the fracture is on the right side, place your left finger in the mouth of the patient; and similarly, if the fracture is on the left side, then use your right finger; and press against the swelling of the fracture from within with ease to the outer parts, with your other hand from the outer part of the bone; do this gently.
And if the jaw fracture is already separated into two parts, then it is necessary to apply extension from both sides as much as possible to achieve proper alignment.
And if there is a disruption with the teeth, then strengthen those that you expect will break with a thread of gold or silver or silk; and place on the broken jaw a piece of cloth soaked in cerot, and then place on it a folded cloth, and place on it a large piece of soft cloth cut appropriately, or a pad of dough equal to the length of the jaw; and bind this above according to how you have prepared the bandage for it and as is convenient for its application, so that it is not destroyed.
And command the patient to use rest and calm, and give him food in the form of thin soups.
And if you find that the shape has already been altered in some way, then you should change and loosen it on the third day; and then correct what is already altered of it with a plaster made from powdered linseed, egg albumen, or ground chickpeas, after having removed the cerot, and place over the plaster a thin layer of linen.
And then as you place and adjust the plaster on it, if the position of the bone is not altered, leave it so that you do not loosen it until it is healed and the fracture has strengthened; for this fracture typically becomes strong in about three weeks.
And if another abscess occurs that requires cauterization, then administer what we have said for other similar cases in the treatment of it until it is healed, and let that abscess be treated accordingly.
And if the fracture is a compound fracture, now consider whether the bone fragment is displaced or not; then use your skill to extract those fragments with what is appropriate for their removal using the instruments.
And if the opening of the wound is narrow, now enlarge it with a scalpel according to the amount that you deem necessary.
And when you have extracted those fragments that need to be removed, if the wound opening is wide, then close it; and if not, apply one of the plasters appropriate for this condition until it heals.
Chapter 5.
On the restoration of the collarbone when it is broken.
There are various ways the collarbone can be broken in front of the muscle, and this fracture occurs in one of three manners.
For it may be broken, or displaced, or separated into two parts without any fragments being displaced; and this is easier for its restoration. Or it may be such that fragments are displaced within it, making its restoration difficult. Or it may be such that the fracture is a compound fracture.
And the procedure in such a case, when the fracture is a compound fracture, is that you have two assistants present, so that one holds an instrument and stabilizes the broken collarbone, and the other extends the arm over that area.
To set the fracture, apply the splints in such a way that the shape is made according to what is necessary, and let there not be too much prominence or concavity.
And if you need more extension, then it is necessary to place under the armpit of the injured side a ball made of cloth or wool, and its size should be according to your requirement; extend and elevate the shoulder and compress the ball with your hand so that you set the fracture as needed.
And if you cannot pull the end of the shoulder blade to the outer parts, so that it reaches its proper place, then it is necessary for the patient to be reclined on his back, and you should place something under the muscle on one side, about halfway in size.
And the assistant should press the muscle on the lower parts so that the bone of the shoulder blade is elevated to its proper place, and then you should correct the fracture and set it with the splints mentioned.
But if you feel splinters of the shoulder blade and small pieces, then it is necessary to cut them out and remove those splinters with ease.
And if it is stuck in the bone, then you should devise an incision with one of the instruments that you have prepared for this purpose, after placing under the shoulder blade the instrument used by the surgeon; and this tool is made of wood or iron and has the following form:
And it resembles the bottom of a bowl, with its concave shape; and its width should be according to your need for it, depending on the size of the bone or its smallness.
Make it as long as is appropriate for the operation; let it have two ends, just as you wish, and one end be broad and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the wound which you incised when you extracted the splinter from the bone is wide, and you are sure it will form an abscess, then you now adjust the two lips of the wound with a suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear it may form an abscess soon, then you should employ the wound with dressings and powders according to the quantity of the wound's disruption.
And if an abscess forms soon, then insert a dressing soaked in vinegar and rose water, and place it over that.
And do not let the splint be loose over the broken bone; and therefore it is necessary that you see the patient every day, so when the bandaging has softened or you see the splint already loosened, then you should correct this and tighten the bandage.
And place the patient's pillow over their neck, and below it place some bolsters so that during sleep the head is raised slightly; therefore raise the support from the side of the injury, and thus elevate the collarbone fracture with the muscle elevation; or bind the arm to the neck.
And do not loosen the bandage unless it is necessary due to an abscess, after twelve days.
And then renew the plaster if you see that the face of it needs it, and adjust in the constriction and restore it, and leave it until it is restored and the collarbone fracture is set.
And it is strengthened and comforted according to the method in twenty-eight days, and sometimes in some people it is done in less time.
Chapter VI.
On the restoration of a shoulder fracture.
The collarbone is broken in the middle part and not at the ends, only the edges.
And when it is broken at that location — and it is broken in the middle part — and it is known only by palpation, then according to the shape of the fracture, prepare yourself and reduce it according to its natural shape in every way that is possible for you.
And thus place on the wound powder of ground egg whites and rose petals; and next place a double-layered cloth pad; and then place over it, according to the quantity of the spoon, all or at least a little bit, of the lotion.
And if the lotion leaves behind cavities in some places according to the spoon, then you fill those cavities with lint pads, and then apply the lotion evenly.
And then secure above with a bandage made of linen strips, applying it with sufficient firmness to hold the lotion in place without moving it from its location.
And examine the bandage each day; and whenever it needs to be loosened, change it and tighten it evenly if the lotion has moved from its place.
And apply the medication on its proper side.
And the spoon is restored in xx days or in .xxv.
When this number is complete, then you loosen the bandage, and know for certain that those bones from which there was no fear of their beginning are dissolved.
And if you proceed to the place of the bone piece and place the ligature below it, then you cut above it, remove it, and do as we have said regarding the fracture of the clavicle or suppuration caused by a cauterization should this happen.
Chapter VII.
On the restoration of foot fractures.
The foot sometimes is broken in the middle and remains immobile; however, its extremities move as cicadas do, moving slightly and repeatedly.
And the symptoms when the middle of it is broken are that it tilts towards the inner parts and there is great pain, difficulty in movement, coughing, shortness of breath, and vomiting blood; and a cavity forms in the broken bone and this is not hidden from sensation.
And the restoration of it is that the patient should lie supine on their back, and place a bolster between their shoulders; then gently press the muscles, and adjust the ribs using more than two fingers, and carefully proceed with the operation and alignment in every possible manner suitable for this, until the figure of the bone returns as required.
And then place on it a plaster and wool, and put under a delicate wooden splint or a similar light material, then wrap that in cloth; and then carefully bind it over the broken bone without pressing too hard; and proceed with bandaging around the back several times, and tighten that binding appropriately; and then examine the bandage at all times, and when it has loosened, tighten it again.
And if necessity compels the removal of that which has been corroded or protruded or caused pain or an abscess in that place, then cut and loosen that which is there and remove the plaster and correct what has happened by this method of treatment; and immediately apply the plaster again if you wish to do so; and adjust the stricture until it is healed.
Chapter VIII.
On the restoration of ribs when they are broken.
Know that the fracture of ribs does not occur except in places where they join together; and at their extremities near the front, there is no breakage of those parts because they are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of it is not hidden from the feeling of those parts during the examination of them.
And the restoration should be such that it equalizes the fracture according to the possible method, until it heals as necessary, and then apply plaster and bind the broken bone with a splint if that is needed.
And if the fracture of the ribs inclines towards the inner parts, then severe pain and piercing sensation occur, as in the case of someone who has wept, because the bone punctures the membrane; and breathing difficulties, coughing, and vomiting of blood may also happen; and this is difficult to cure.
And the Ancients were skilled in that with powerful methods.
And what they say is: it is necessary to pause from foods that produce inflammation and gas, so that the belly becomes inflated and extended, and the fracture is pushed outward.
Let us proceed with what will not be a cause for easily generating an inflamed abscess if it does not occur; and if it has already occurred, now help with that.
And some of them say that it should be placed above the wound and close to where it is drawn strongly; and this is similar to reasoning, but it is feared that the wound will draw the superfluities to the place due to weakness.
And some of them say: let the place be covered with wool that has already been submerged in hot oil, and let powders be placed between the ribs until it is full, so that the bandage wraps around as closely to that roundness; and then treat the illness with the cure for pleurisy using food and medicine.
And if the illness is caused by violent constraint that cannot be tolerated, and the bone pushes through the membrane at a puncture point, we fear for the condition; now it should be that we cut above the location, expose the broken rib, and then place over it an instrument to secure the incision previously prepared, and that they cut the bone with ease, and extract it.
And then we adjust the two edges of the wound, if it is large, with stitching, and treat it with plasters until it heals.
And if an abscess occurs internally, now you should apply heat and infuse powders in reddened oil and place it on the area, and treat the illness with what is appropriate for an internal abscess.
And let them lie supine on the side on which they sleep until they are healed.
Chapter IX.
On the restoration of fractures of the second vertebra and the neck.
In some cases, fractures occur in the bones of the neck, and similarly in the vertebrae of the back. And when this happens to someone and you want to know if they will recover or not, at that moment consider the following: if you see their fingers already softened, numb, and lifeless, unable to move them or stretch them, and when you press on them with your fingers or pierce them with a needle and there is no feeling of pain in them, then you know that they will not recover, and in most cases it is fatal.
And if they can move them and feel constriction when pressed on and pricked, then you know that the vertebra is safe and that the patient will recover with treatment.
And if the vertebrae of the neck are similar to this condition and you want to know if they will recover or not, then at that moment consider their breathing.
If you see that they are already weakened, the same applies to them as it would to their limbs. Soon after lying on their back, they experience vomiting and involuntary diarrhea, and sometimes when lying on their back, they want to urinate but cannot, then you know that it is fatal, and at that point, there is no need to work on treating them.
But if none of those symptoms occur, the cause is better and less dangerous.
And the treatment for them is to apply poultices to reduce the swelling on the affected vertebra with olive oil or boiled honey; place this on that area several times a day until the swelling subsides.
During the hours of rest, one of the soothing and beneficial plasters should be applied, and it should be wrapped with a bandage; and order the patient to use repose and tranquility and not to sleep on the side where pain is felt until it is healed.
And if it happens that the flesh around the bone becomes detached or for any cause that it is separated from it, then you should cover it and hold the bone; and immediately join the two edges of the wound, with sutures if necessary; and afterwards treat it with some incarnative ointment until it is healed.
But if the end of the bone is broken and it is the tip of the bone, then you should insert your fingers into the buttock with your left hand, and align the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible by second-degree reduction; and afterwards apply a plaster and splint, if necessary, and wrap it.
And if you feel detached and broken flesh in it, then you should act on that, remove it, and treat the wound with the treatment that is prescribed until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
On the restoration of the broad (or wide) area.
The bones of the broad areas are often broken, and when they break, the fracture does not extend beyond their extremities but rather is incised along their length and deviates to the sides; this causes pain in the affected area and puncture sensations, and movement is impaired when fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you should place your hand on it so that it extends over the fracture in a manner that corresponds to its figure.
And if the fracture is at the extremity of the limb, then you should align it according to what seems appropriate to you so that it resembles its natural shape.
And if the fracture is in the long bone that inclines toward the lower parts, then you should have the patient lie on their belly so that you can prepare the restoration of that fracture. And when you have aligned it, apply a plaster over it, and then place a splint of wood or cane beside it, and bind it tightly with bandages in such a way that you do not fear any displacement of the fracture or movement of the splint.
And align the concavity so that it fills appropriately, and command the patient to sleep on their back or on their side so that it maintains alignment properly.
And if an abscess occurs suddenly, then you should stop the extension and restoration of it until the abscess is treated, and apply what is appropriate for that condition as it has been done before; and afterward return to the restoration of it, and bind it as is necessary.
And if the bones are displaced or crushed, then you should treat them.
Chapter XI.
On the restoration of the fracture of the wrist.
The adjutori is what is between the shoulder blade and the top of the shoulder.
And if it is fractured, then the restoration of it is in one of two ways, one of which is that you take a flat piece of wood of moderate thickness in this form:
Let there be two ends of bandages, and let it be suspended from an elevated place.
Place the patient on a board; then lay the broken arm outside onto the stick until it reaches the midpoint where the curve of the stick is attached.
And then suspend something heavy from it, or have the patient extend it downward with their own strength; and then the physician should bring the fracture back to its proper position, as needed.
And another method is to have the patient lie on their back with their neck raised and suspend the head by the neck with bandages; then instruct two assistants, one of whom holds above the fracture near it, and the other below the patient, each pulling in their own direction.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then apply bandages over the fracture on one side and over the other side with another bandage, and have each of the two assistants pull in their respective directions.
And if the fracture is near the end of the muscle, then it is necessary to place half of the bandage over the knot and the other part below the fracture towards the joint.
And similarly, if the fracture is near the joint, then it is necessary to place the bandage over that location and around the joint.
Tie it in such a way that it is comfortable and easy to work with; then when it is tied appropriately as needed and applied properly, tighten it if no inflammation or abscess occurs.
If an abscess occurs after cauterization, if there is no constriction within seven days, then place a linen strip soaked in vinegar and rose oil so that the abscess can rest, and then tighten it.
And the method of tightening that is to place a plaster over the fracture, and then place a wrapping of wet cloth over the plaster; and then adjust the arm on that support, and place its hand on its own muscle.
And place the cloth and tighten over the support for the arm so that the arm is in a fixed position, if there is no cause from the broken bone prohibiting this.
And now use a splint.
And it is that you place on the fracture that splint which is thicker and stronger than the other splints; and place between each splint the width of a finger, and let the length of the splints be according to the break plus an addition of three fingers from all sides.
And then tighten over the splints with the bandage which I have described at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your bandage over the site of the fracture be tighter there. And the farther apart the fracture is, the looser the bandaging should be.
And so if you position the splints and the bandaging as we have said at the time when you restore the limb to its proper position, then do it.
And if you fear an abscess after cauterization, then leave the bandaging and the splints for seven days as we have said.
Therefore, consider the bandage in all parts for three days so that no pus or inflammation occurs; or it is forbidden for food to reach the member due to excess fluid retention or constriction; and thus correct everything as we have said.
But if you are confident that no cause of this will occur, then do not loosen the bandage for at least three days or more.
And let the patient lie on their back, and their head should be raised slightly to rest above their stomach.
And place a pad filled with wool on the injured area, and at all times of the night or day, ensure that the shape of the broken limb is not loosened or dissolved, nor should the bandage be too loose; thus, correct this all with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order we mentioned earlier, such that the food is light at first until healing begins; then it should be gradually made heavier.
For the custom of the bandage and changing it is that they be tight for forty days; then they should be loosened, a bath should be given, and medicine applied with substances that correct this.
And if the fracture is still rough and rubbing against itself, then do not loosen the bandage, and keep the splints in place for one day or up to two months.
Chapter XII.
On the Restoration of a Fractured Arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called the ulna and radius; one is small and lies under the little finger, while the other is larger and is situated below the smaller one, towards the thumb side.
If only the larger bone or the smaller one is broken, and firmly set together.
And when the smaller bone above is broken, its restoration is easy, and its healing is quicker.
And when the bone below is broken, the fracture is severe, and its healing is difficult.
And worse still is when two bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the smaller one above, it is appropriate for the surgeon, due to its restoration, to allow a brief extension period with ease until it becomes level.
But if the larger bone is the one that is broken, then a more vigorous extension period should be allowed.
And if both bones are broken together, then it is necessary to allow an even more vigorous extension period.
And it is necessary to place the figure of the hand due to its restoration and extension spread out on a board, with the wrist above all the fingers, such that the thumb is higher than the other fingers, and the little finger is lower than the others, lying flat on a sloped surface.
And there should be a board in front of it at an appropriate height or straight ahead, so that the little finger does not have to work.
And one assistant should extend the arm from below with the patient's hand or with a sling, and another assistant should extend it from above similarly.
And the surgeon should then place the bone in such a way that it assumes the best shape possible.
And if in the treatment of the bone you encounter difficulties, then at this point you should try to reduce all parts to their proper place with your skill.
And if a separate difficulty appears that makes the condition worse and is not to your hope for its restoration, then you should act upon it and remove that part according to the method we have mentioned above.
But if the fracture is due to plague, I have already treated that in a separate chapter, so follow the treatment described there.
And if an abscess occurs at the beginning of your restoration, then you should line a cloth with cerot made from rose oil and white wax, which should be between thickness and subtlety, and place a strong cloth over it with fine stitching so that the abscess can subside; and then remove the cerot and apply a plaster prepared from powdered mallow in hound’s tongue juice, and afterwards place poultices; and let the poultice placed over that fracture be more distant and stronger.
And know that the number of poultices for the arm varies in major treatments, or rather, whether the fracture is in one bone or in two bones together.
And then place your stitching over the location of the fracture more firmly and vigorously, and when it is most necessary to apply compression above the fracture or below it, place the stitches slightly looser according to how it was described at the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths that you wrap around the fracture should be soft and moistened cloths, not hard or rough, and the thread with which they are sewn should be of medium thickness between fine and coarse, as we have recommended.
And consider the limb and the bandaging for some days. And if any cause arises for which correction is necessary, as quickly as possible after such an occurrence to the limb, you should bathe the limb with hot water until that condition subsides.
And do not force the limb until it rests, and then return to the binding.
And if the bandaging has already been loosened and the bone is already set and appears to be in place, then correct everything with your skill.
And consider whether the nutrition is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive bandaging, for then you should loosen that a little and leave it for some days until nutrition reaches it, and then strengthen that.
But if the patient does not suffer from any of the causes we have mentioned, then it is not necessary to loosen it for about 20 days or thereabouts.
And then let the patient's arm be suspended by their own skill of active movement, and make them lie down on their back to sleep.
And know that this fracture is restored in about 30 days or 32 days, or sometimes it may be restored in 28 days, and all of this depends on the condition of the tissues and the strength of the patient.
Chapter XIII.
On the restoration of a fracture of the wrist and the fingers.
Know that at the wrist and the joints of the fingers, when there is a fracture, it usually heals in about two months.
When it happens that the arm is fractured or dislocated, at that time it is necessary for the patient to lie flat and facing upwards according to equality, and place the arm above in such an extended position.
And then the minister should extend the broken bones and the physician should adjust them until they are properly set.
And now it is necessary to apply a plaster and cotton, unless an abscess occurs, in which case cauterize; and then place a splint above according to the size of the area, and you have already wrapped that part in linen.
But if the fracture is in the parts inside the palm of the hand, now make an appearance of a sphere with cloth, and command the patient to hold that with their fractured palm; then bind it tightly with cloth.
And let the splint be made of wood and wrapped in cloth in which it is raised, so that the wood fits against the palm; and bind it as necessary.
But if the fracture is in the parts outside, now it is necessary to place a splint above and another splint below on the palm, so that the arm is supported and straight; and then apply the bandage, as you would wrap the arm at the wrist with linen.
And if there is a fracture in one of the bones of the fingers, now if it is a thumb, adjust it as necessary and then bind it to the palm; and if you wish, place a small straight splint so that the fracture is elevated and not worsened.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as in the middle finger, or the ring finger, or the little finger, now it should be adjusted and bandaged to its corresponding finger; or all should be bound together continuously, which is better; or place a small splint on that finger, as we have said for the thumb.
And consider, in your restoration, and approach that, the disposition of abscesses cut and opposed — both the hand operation — both with what is needed when some cause of those occurs, as it is repeated the narration of them.
Chapter XIV.
On the restoration of a fracture of the collarbone.
The collarbone is often fractured and is felt according to how it has shifted forward and upward.
And the restoration of it is done in such a way that one bandage is stretched over the fracture and another bandage below the fracture, and the patient lies face down on their belly.
And the practitioner should extend all the bandages to their proper position according to the quality when the fracture is in the middle part of the bone.
But if it is closer to the shoulder joint, then the bandage should be placed close to the armpit at that part, so that the extension can take place there, and the other bandage below the fracture.
And similarly, if the fracture is closer to the neck, then the bandage should be placed close to the neck, and the extension should take place at the parts below.
And the physician should align the bone with both of his hands until it is reduced according to its natural appearance and the bone is joined by proper union; then it is necessary to apply plaster and bandaging, unless an abscess occurs in the limb. But if an abscess does occur, then you should leave it alone for several days until the abscess has subsided, and then return to your treatment.
The binding of the splint should be done as follows: wrap around the fracture a hard bandage, and fold it two or three times, and trim off the excess; then bind the pad so that it reaches to the ankle and to the base of the foot.
And insert a strip of cloth between the skin and the pad near the end towards the bottom, so that the ends of the cloth extend beyond both sides of the pad, and then wrap around the pad and the limb whatever excess remains of the bandage.
And then place over the limb at the site of the fracture splints, and place one strip over the bone of the pad.
And then fill the spaces between the pad and the limb with soft cloths, so that the binding is snug.
And then begin the binding tightly on top at the site of the fracture with three or four turns, and as you move away from the area of the fracture with the bandage, your binding should be looser and lighter and softer.
And then extend the two ends of the strip towards the bottom until it reaches the ankle, and tie both ends of the splints from the other side so that the bandage does not come loose from its place.
And then leave the binding in place, so that it does not cause corrosion, abscess, swelling, or similar issues to the limb; and if any such causes occur, then undo part of it and correct whatever has occurred as soon as possible, as we have said before.
If there is pus in the bone which must be removed, then you should scrape that thoroughly as much as possible; and if not, open it up and remove what can be reached, and treat with whatever remedy precedes until it is healed.
And in some cases, this fracture of the skull is strengthened by these bandages that are attached to it, as we have said about the auxiliary and the arm.
However, in the restoration of the limb in such a manner made, no bad limping occurs to the patient; and if it is restored alone, or that which is applied to the other side, then it is necessary that the person limp for some time.
And know that the skull is strengthened in forty days or a few more days, and all of this depends on the diversity of constitutions and other conditions.
Chapter XV.
On the restoration of the fracture of the thumb bone.
Know that the fracture of the thumb bone tends to happen at the tip, but it also occurs in other places frequently.
However, if there is a fracture, it is often merely a crack or contusion in its parts, and this is either one pain or several pains, and all of this depends on the sensation.
And the restoration of it is such that it should be scraped and joined back together its broken parts as described, until it is aggregated and conjoined as much as possible by equality, ease, and fabrication.
And then apply a plaster and place over it a round bandage if needed; and strengthen it above with proper compression.
And so consider all the positions which we have recommended for other fractures, as well as abscesses and similar conditions, in such a way that they are appropriate to every event and suitable to what is necessary within them until they are healed.
Chapter XVI.
On the restoration of the fracture of the hip.
Know that in the hip there are two bones, one of which is thick and is called the hip bone; the other is slender and is called the femur.
And accidents of certain types of fracture occur to both bones as happen to the two bones of the arm, and thus restoration is performed on it similarly to the restoration of the arm, and the operation for it is the same.
And if two bones are broken, the hip is affected in all parts.
And if the more slender bone is broken, the hip is affected in the forward parts.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you will find it below, and it is of those causes that are not within your control.
And then administer extension and reduction and fixation of the fragments accordingly.
However, be careful if the fracture of the hip is severe and has many stages, that the extension be less and more gradual, and proceed with caution according to your skill.
And in the case of the hip, there is a reason to operate as you would on the arm, which is when you equalize the fragments and complete the entire procedure. At this time, you will take pieces of wood from plants that grow near causes that are smooth and not thorny; choose those with small grooves, and they should not be from thorny varieties. The length should be according to the length of the hip, and the thickness should be proportionate to what is below.
And wrap each cloth in two coverings according to its length, and place one at the convex part of one side and the other at the concave part of the other side, and let it be tied into the deepest part of the wound.
And tie the two rods in three places at both ends and at the middle point, for with this structure the arch is prevented from leaning to the right or left, and it stands in good position.
And a piece of wood has already been administered according to the length of the arch; and place the arch so that it maintains its mobility.
And especially do this in the case of a fracture, and when it is properly a condition requiring such treatment.
And every day consider the arch and study it carefully based on previous studies, and you must guard it from abscesses and inflammation or other similar causes; and when any such cause arises, then act accordingly until it is healed.
Chapter XVII.
Of the fracture of the heel and of the bones of the foot and of the aforementioned.
A complete fracture does not often occur in the heel; but fractures can happen occasionally in the bones of the foot; and among those of the foot, few suffer fractures, and they rarely experience anything more than strain due to their disposition.
And if the bones of the foot suffer a fracture and these bones are elevated above their natural position, then the patient should rest his foot on prepared ground as described.
