And then place something on top of that which is raised from those bones, and press down on them, and bind them so that they return to their places.
And then apply a plaster above it and place something above that, and below the chest of the patient place a small table with two flat surfaces.
And then strengthen that limb with a proper structure, since you have wrapped it in cloths and other materials as needed.
And as three or four days pass, then you loosen the bandage, for you will find the bones bound, either joined together or dislocated, and the treatment in such cases is as you know.
But if some say they are joined together, then you should restore them and bind them as I recommend to you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. And then place a splint under the joined finger according to the length of the finger, and leave some space around it; and below the patient place the table as recommended to you, and strengthen it with proper support.
But if what is joined together involves two or three or more bones, then you should place a splint of appropriate size wrapped in linen cloth on each finger, and strengthen the table at the base of the patient’s body so that it rests on one of the flat surfaces of the table for proper support across the chest.
And it is important for you not to forget that you should consider all the causes mentioned to you in the treatment of other fractures and accidents that we have discussed; and prepare yourself for every condition with what is necessary.
Chapter Eighteen.
On the fracture of the skull of a woman and of the bone of the penis and of the virile member of a man.
When the skull of a woman is fractured, at that time make that incision in the area where it has occurred, and then slightly incline it towards the part of the back.
And then the midwife should fill the skull with cotton, and it should be done in such a way that it conforms to the shape of the head; and then the woman should be bandaged, and her back slightly elevated little by little, because all this is done so that before the fracture site, it remains as it was and the fractured bone returns to its place.
And then place poultices on her back, and when she wants to urinate, remove the cotton to facilitate it, and after she urinates, reduce and return that as per the method by which you mixed it at the beginning, and then return to her initial poultice.
And do this for seven days or around that time, because it takes that long to restore.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder and sew onto it a tube made of cane; then insert the entire bladder into the skull fracture site, and then blow air through the tube until the bladder is inflated inside the head, because this helps the fracture to return to its proper position.
And then fill it with cotton and keep it for several days as we have recommended, until it is healed.
And when the bone of the penis in a man or woman is fractured, at that time administer for its restoration and alignment what we have recommended for the hip bone.
In what is true and not hidden in these nearby fractures that rarely occur, especially for one who has some study and use and understands my book; for that fracture signifies to you in the major dispositions by the true method in the restoration and in the tightening of it, and thus understands well.
In the penis of the man when it is grasped, take a bandage made of cloth, and put the penis in that; then place around it a wrapping of cloth, and let it be wrapped up, and afterwards let it be left for three days or about that time, and it will be healed.
Chapter XIX.
On the restoration of bone fractures when they are inflamed.
It is appropriate for one to whom it happens that there is a fracture with inflammation, and especially if the bone is large like the bone of the thigh or the humerus, or similar to those, that you treat and bleed them immediately if the conditions for bleeding indicate this as we have said above.
And if the inflammation causes bleeding, now it is appropriate that you treat for retention of it by such means as you powder on it finely powdered eggshell, if no other cause is present.
And then begin to restore the fracture on that same day, and do not delay this, unless an abscess occurs.
But if an abscess forms suddenly, now you should delay the restoration of it until the ninth day, until the abscess has subsided, and do not approach it on the third and fourth days thereafter, for you will cause harmful accidents.
If a broken bone protrudes above the joint and is visible, you should proceed to reduce it and align it with ease and minimal extension.
If extension is not within your capability and the alignment requires more than what you have, then you should reduce it with these tools; and this tool is made of iron, its length being seven or eight digits, and its width according to the size of the wound; and therefore it is necessary for the surgeon to take three or four such tools, depending on what is needed for various types of fractures.
And it should be round, and slightly thickened so that it does not bend due to the pressure on it during the operation.
And it should have a sharp tip at which there is a margin on both ends; and the upper part should be thicker, while the middle part should taper towards the sides.
And this is the form of it:
And it is called in Greek "labrum volentz atelarem," which means a small palette.
So that the end of it can be placed steadily with the tip raised above the protruding end of the bone.
And press it in one motion until the bone returns to its place and is aligned with some degree of alignment.
And thus strive to align the ends of the fracture between them, and if the broken end is delicate and does not hold the tool well, then you should insert the end of that bone so that the instrument has a good grip on it.
If you cannot reduce the bone with what we have recommended, then now use that with what is suitable for the knives which we have mentioned; or saw that with one of the saws in whatever way is possible for you.
And remove that which remains in the bone of roughness and small scratches.
And when the bone is reduced, and after the reduction the patient still suffers from severe pain in the tooth, then you will know that the bone has not returned to its natural place; and then do what you consider will be of great benefit to the patient.
When your restoration of the bone is complete, then soak a cloth in black styptic wine, and do this properly even in summer, and do not place cerot or any other substance containing oil on the wound, so that it does not cause rotting and corruption.
And then administer poultices at the appropriate time for your completion of the bone's restoration, and leave the wound uncovered so that you can cut away any protruding parts with forceps and use dressings according to the extent of the wound.
And be careful not to touch the wound at the fracture, for many times unskilled doctors have done this, and it has caused death or incurable illness or severe harm to their patients. And if the swelling is slight, soft, and different from the swelling of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or it is a bad wound, and you fear some of the accidents we have mentioned, and you feel pain in that area, then do not use poultices on it, but instead apply a dressing made of hard cloths rather than poultices and treat it accordingly.
And when one or two days have passed since the wound began to heal, whereupon granulation has started to form, then you remove the cloth that you had placed over it, and immediately apply bandages, using those which according to our custom are used for treating wounds, such as tetradecphas and similar ones to this.
And it is necessary for you to untie the bandage and examine the wound every morning and evening until it is consolidated and healed, and it should be such that the member is prepared in such a way that the pus can drain from it downwards with ease.
If several days have passed since the wound began and it has not consolidated nor has the granulation tissue formed, then you should know that here there is a delay due to small issues, for which you must examine the wound with a probe. And of those delays, those that are separate should be removed and extracted, while those that cannot be separated and cause pain when the member is moved, you should proceed to incise them according to what is possible for you.
And if the wound becomes infected with gangrene or erysipelas, or any other type of corruption and putrefaction, then you should proceed according to one of those methods mentioned in the preceding chapter on the treatment of such conditions.
And regarding this matter in which you must be cautious and present your best judgment: when a large bone is broken, treat the limb as you would the bones of the wrist and forearm and similar ones, and do not attempt traction or extraction if doing so might lead to death after four attempts; leave it inside for pus to drain, as it will likely fall out on its own within twenty or thirty days; and then attend to the wound if it is visible, otherwise leave it be.
Chapter XX.
On the treatment of joint stiffness that occurs as a consequence of some fracture.
This stiffness often occurs in the joints during the healing of a fracture, especially near the joints themselves, and it distorts the shape of the limb; and it greatly prevents the limb from performing its natural functions.
Then consider whether the stiffness is recent and administer medicines that have a binding effect such as aloes, henbane, wormwood, squill, colocynth, and similar ones; take any of these individually or in combination. Confess (administer) them either by mouth with vinegar, or with egg white, or mixed with water, and apply it to the stiffness on linen, and bind it tightly with proper compression.
And let the compression remain for several days without loosening it; then loosen it and reapply another one until the stiffness is alleviated; or apply a thin sheet of lead over it gradually, as lead has the property of reducing inflammation in limbs.
But if the stiffness has already turned to bone and is causing severe pain, necessitating its removal urgently, then cut it at the part that is most affected, and excise the excess or reduce it with some instruments until it is alleviated; and care for it until it heals.
Chapter XXI.
On the restoration of a fracture when it is set and subsequently the limb becomes weak and does not follow its natural course.
When a bone fracture is set, and subsequently the limb becomes weak and frail, this happens for several reasons: one reason is excessive dissolution of bandages and binding done improperly; or due to overly tight compression preventing nourishment from reaching the limb; or due to an excess of unnecessary applications; or due to premature movement not at the appropriate time; or due to a lack of blood in the patient's body and weakness.
For the treatment, give food to the patient and make his heart tender so that the blood multiplies in him; administer baths; and apply cautery and cupping to him.
And apply plasters to the limb, so that the plaster attracts nourishment; or continue rubbing with cold water, until the nourishment flows and returns to its natural form.
Chapter XXII.
On the treatment of broken bones when they have healed incorrectly and prohibit further operation on them.
When it happens that a limb already restored near its healing shows curvature and prominence in the broken bone and knotting, and this is due to the deformation of the shape of the limb; however, since the limb is not prohibited from its natural movement, you should not accept the word of one who believes that member to be newly broken.
But there are indeed false physicians and restorers who have done this in our land; and this operation is a reprehensible one, leading to great fear, the sign of which is fatal.
But if the limping and knotting are recent, then it is necessary to rub them with cold water in which soft herbs are soaked, such as flax seeds and radish roots, and melilot, and similar ones; and a soft plaster should be applied as well, made properly for this purpose; or take mucilage from radish seeds and mix it with flour of linseed or olive oil, then apply it; or grind fat figs with dove’s milk, and similar medicines to those mentioned for consolidating.
In some cases, it is dissolved by constant gentle friction which is applied to the most humid parts, and movement of the member should be administered to all parts at all times.
If the tortuosity has already advanced and has become hard and converted into stone, and it has caused the need for treatment with iron, then it is necessary to let the pus above drain out and the continuation of the bone be dissolved, and to excise what is superfluous from the knotting of that material with the bone with subtle instruments, and facilitate this under careful attention and study.
Chapter XXIII.
On the treatment of dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of any of the joints from its proper place, by which it is prohibited from movement, and the figure of the member is distorted, causing severe pain and great injuries.
When a dislocation occurs, it is necessary to attempt its reduction as soon as possible and not delay in reducing it, for if it is delayed, the joint becomes inflamed and hard, making the reduction of the dislocation difficult.
And for this reason, it is not advisable to move or extend the limb during the period of inflammation, as movement often causes spasm and severe pain.
But when this happens, it is necessary to prepare for the puncture of the abscess, and allow the limb to rest until the inflammation subsides; then bathe the member with warm water and oil, and subsequently reduce it with care.
And treat the entire limb with what will help it remember its proper place.
Here are the bones set in order as previously described for fractures; I now enter into the part dealing with lesser wounds.
Chapter XXIIIV.
On the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
So when both jaws are dislocated and do not function; the dislocation of both can be done in one of two ways: either they are partially removed from their positions with slight movement, and slightly softened; or they are completely dislocated and fully displaced, to the point where they are softened at the joint where saliva flows, and where he cannot hold anything nor close his mouth, and his tongue is wrapped around his words.
But when the dislocation is slight, it often returns to its proper position on its own with little cause.
But if the dislocation is complete and full, it must be reduced immediately, and should not be delayed at all.
And it is that the minister hold the head of the patient, and the physician insert his thumb into the root of the dislocated jaw inside the mouth if the dislocation is on one side; or he inserts both thumbs together if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers should be outside equally; and orders the patient to cease moving their jaws and relax them on each side; and the physician then guides the jaw back into place until it returns to its proper position.
And if the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated together, then administer a warm mixture of water and oil to ease the reduction of both, and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And so when returning and using bandages, and the mouth of the wound is closed and not softened, at that time pause on those powders of cloth with wax, and let it be made of wax and rubbed oil; and then let it be applied with ease to the soft parts.
And let the patient sleep on his back, and his head be placed between his two ears, so that it does not turn to the right or left; and do not force him to chew for any reason; let his food be given in liquid form until such time as when the pains have subsided and his jaws are loosened, then allow him to eat what he wishes.
And this should be administered with ease, and do not work on the opening of the mouth for eating and drinking and other uses until such time as the jaw is loosened and healed.
And if the reduction of both jaws when they are dislocated at any time and not returned to their places is difficult, then fevers often occur with frequent sweat, and easily the patient's belly becomes swollen, and easily vomiting of pure bile occurs.
And when you see this, then you know that it is hopeless; and often the one to whom this happens dies within ten days.
Chapter XXV.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the clavicle and the extremity of the muscle.
The clavicle is not dislocated from the chest inward towards the body; and sometimes it is dislocated outward, and this can be felt.
And for the restoration of the hand, the patient lies on his back and extends his arms. And then press the area with your palm firmly, for it will return.
And then apply the plaster and powder to that area.
But the extremity of that which joins the spine and is continuous with it does not become dislocated in any day; therefore, it must be reduced and bandaged as we have said and according to how you have prepared it.
And then apply the plaster, powder, and bandage, and command the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until he is healed.
And in this method of treatment, the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter XXVI.
On the Reduction of Muscle Dislocation.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated except in three ways, one of which is when it is dislocated from the upper part to the lower part.
And the second is when it is dislocated at the upper part of the muscle, and this happens slowly; and it does not become dislocated at the rear parts by the shoulder; and it does not become dislocated at the front parts by the nerves.
And it is most often dislocated and protruded at the lower parts below the joint, and more particularly in those whose flesh is thin, it easily becomes dislocated; but in those with thick muscles, it protrudes with difficulty.
If it happens that any person is struck or falls and the muscle of the place becomes swollen with swelling, and it is treated with what has already been displaced.
Then proceed with that which you have placed upon it, and now proceed with the treatment of it.
And the dislocation of it is known when it is in the lower parts away from the tendon; therefore, distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the normal muscle, for you will notice clear differences among them; you will find a concavity at the head of the muscle and below the tendon there is contact with the head of the muscle as if by someone, and the injured person cannot move their hand to their ear, nor can they use that limb in any movement.
If it is dislocated from the feet or above, then you will immediately notice a clear touch, and it is not concealed.
And this dislocation is gently reduced when it is fresh and the injured person is a child.
And the reduction of it is that the practitioner raises the hand of the injured above the parts; then he places his fingers below the tendon and elevates the joint with strength to its proper place, and the practitioner raises the hand of the injured, and extends it properly; then he holds it still as it returns gently.
But if it does not return as we have said and after several days from the beginning of the dislocation, then the injured person should be bathed in hot water and administered a lotion to soften and smooth it as if for burns, using fenugreek, melilot in water, and it should be applied.
Let the patient lie supine on their back and place under them a pillow of wool that is even between softness and firmness. Then the surgeon places his hand on the sphere and lifts the muscle upward, and holds the patient's arm so it is not injured in the process, and another assistant should hold the patient’s head so it is not turned to either side, for it immediately returns to its position.
And if you wish, reduce that second in this manner: have present a man who is taller than the patient and station him on the side of that flank. Insert your hand under the patient’s muscle below the swelling and lift the swelling up toward the head so the patient is suspended in air, and another assistant should hold the patient's arm from behind their abdomen. But if the patient is light, then it is necessary to have something else by which they can be suspended, so that the weight will hold them firmly, for the dislocation returns immediately otherwise.
And it is reduced in this other manner: drive a long pole into the ground with its top rounded like a pestle’s head, neither too thick nor too thin. Place the patient below and have them stand along the length of the pole. Then extend one arm to the side opposite the pole, and firmly extend the rest of their body; the joint will quickly return to its place.
And if the reduction of it is difficult due to all the causes we have mentioned, then use this treatment: take a pole whose length is two yards, width four fingers, and thickness gradually tapering according to the form of the swelling as follows:
And upon the head place linen cloths so that the flesh does not injure the patient.
And let the navel of the patient be pressed down, and let the hand or arm be extended over the body inside the parts below, and let the body be bandaged around the helpers and the arm and the extremity of the hand.
And let the arm be placed on a ladder according to the latitude, and the hand be extended below the parts, and the other side be suspended from the other side, because the joint will soon follow.
And when the instrument is complete according to each manner that is possible for you, then it is necessary that you place below the navel a pad of wool of equal quantity; and afterward place a plaster prepared with powder of burnt eggshells and albumen of eggs over all the muscle, as mentioned above, and then strengthen below the navel with appropriate bandaging; and the bandage should be wrapped around the plaster, and the hand should be attached to the neck, and left alone, and do not disturb the hand for seven days.
And it is necessary that the patient's food be small until the member is strengthened, for it is easier for their recovery.
And then food should be given more after seven or five days, and exercise one movement; and thus if it is firm and not softened, now it is healed.
But if the joint is dislocated due to swelling that occurs or for some other reason, then it is necessary to administer cautery with three tractions as mentioned earlier in the chapter on cautery.
And if all this is done and after about seven days the bandage is removed and the joint has not healed, and the plaster and strapping are reduced over several applications, and it cannot be fixed, and he cannot raise that arm to the upper parts, then you know that the nerves of it, which run to the head of the muscle, have either been cut or stretched or weakened, and understand that the joint will never heal in its proper place.
Regarding the dislocation that occurs from the feet and the hip or the posterior parts, the reduction is performed with impulse and extension as much as necessary until it returns.
And let the other strapping be applied to it, and care for it until it is healed.
And if heaviness and slowness in the movement occur near recovery, then the patient should use baths several times until that heaviness is alleviated and it returns to its original nature.
Chapter XXVII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the neck.
Know that the joint of the neck is dislocated with difficulty and returns with difficulty; and it can be dislocated in all directions, but mostly toward the front or back. And this dislocation does not hide from you when it lies beneath the skin or by touch according to its figure, and when you adjust the dislocated neck, that is made clear to you, because the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm nor reach the muscle's usual place.
And it is necessary that you treat the neck, and reduce the dislocation as soon as possible before it becomes inflamed, because if it becomes inflamed, reduction will be difficult, and even after a long time it may not heal completely. And especially when it is in the posterior parts, for it is worse than all other types of dislocations, more painful, and often leads to death.
And the restoration of it is such that if it is possible to reduce it, then the hand should be extended beyond the two fingers, and the arm should be expanded, and the physician's hands should be above the joint below it, and he should push the joint with his fingertips or with the palm of his hand until it returns to its place.
If the dislocation is to the front parts, then it returns thus: bend the hand with its fingers, so that he strikes the joint with the palm of his hand.
And if the dislocation does not obey reduction, then administer vigorous extension and strong movement.
And it is that two ministers should extend the arm, and they should hold it thus with two ministers so that it is not bent due to the extension.
And it should be rotated the arm in all directions, since it is wrapped in folded cloth long over the hand, or bandage.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, anoint it with some oil so that this helps in the lubricity of the joint for ease; and then push the joint with strong pressure until it returns.
And after the reduction, it is necessary for the patient to rest on a plaster bandage that has stipticity and support with an eagle's claw position, and be wrapped tightly, and the arm should be suspended from the neck, and left like this for several days.
And then it should be loosened; and if the joint is formed in its place, then you loosen the bandage of it and leave it like that.
And if you see that the joint should not be stitched, then apply the plaster and bandage again, and leave it like that for several days until it is healed; and afterwards remove it.
And if it happens that there is great pain during the reduction of the joint and its position is in motion, then administer humidification through bathing and gentle friction, and compression until it is calmed; or place upon the joint something immobile, which is called an immobile support; and afterwards bind it, and leave it on the bone day and night. And afterwards remove that, and place it in a bath; and when it is dry, then you should rub the joint with moderate friction, and afterwards apply an immobile support again, which is called a hard bandage, once, and the second time, and the third time, in the bathing instrument until it is finished.
And if you wish, place a humid and heated cow's hide on that wound and stitch it; and do this for several times, as it is moist and helps to restore its position.
Chapter XXVIII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the wrist joint, which is between the arm and the hand.
The wrist joint sometimes becomes dislocated, and the reduction of this dislocation is easy, but the opposite is true for other joints. Nevertheless, it is necessary to act with speed in reducing it at the moment when it is dislocated, before swelling occurs or an abscess forms.
Here is the translation:
However, it must be considered whether the palm is already stretched over the belly of the hand. If the injury has occurred, then place the back of the patient's hand on a table for extension and reduction; and if the dislocation was before placing both hands down, then position the hand intrinsically on the table so that the doctor's hand can act directly upon that proximity of the joint.
And if it returns immediately, that is good; but otherwise, then strengthen that area with a bandage covering the abscess and leave it like that; and do not repeat this action, for otherwise, it will neither suffer nor allow the reduction of the pus over the days.
However, the joint moves according to its tortuousness; and if you notice in the patient no cause for pain, except that the hand is softened and cannot bear any weight; then know that the nerves are either crushed or severed, and at this point do not apply anything but cauterize it properly, and strengthen it so as not to cause further injury.
And when it has been properly returned, then place on the bandage which we have recommended, and let it be left and strengthened for five days; and afterward, it should be loosened and left, with the hand of the patient being exercised.
However, if the movement of the hand is restricted and you encounter any severity in it, then apply that area with warm water and friction several times until it is relieved.
Chapter XXIX.
On the Treatment of the Dislocation of the Said Joints.
Several times the said joints may become completely dislocated.
When a finger is dislocated at the joint nearest the palm or at the middle joint, hold the finger still and gently push the dislocation back into place.
And then hold the tip of the finger, and suspend it above the part that is dislocated, and leave it like this for two days.
And then suspend it and extend it until it is properly bandaged on that day.
And when it is night, wrap it as described, and do not stop unwrapping it during the day to exercise its movement, and wrap it at night; and do this for several days until it is strong.
And do the same if it is dislocated at the wrist, and wrap it in this manner and perform the first operation like this until it is healed.
And do the same when it is dislocated at other parts as well.
Chapter XXX.
On the treatment of dislocation of the vertebrae in the back.
When a complete dislocation occurs in one of the vertebrae of the back or neck and the vertebrae are moved from their positions, then there is no treatment for this, as death is feared for the patient.
The sign of this is that the patient's defecation occurs without their will, and they cannot control it; often, some parts of their body become weak or two limbs on one side or both arms, or one of these; or a vertebra is displaced from its position, as sometimes it shifts and sometimes it moves to four different directions.
Thus it is described for the parts behind and is called jellyfish-like.
The treatment for it is that you consider whether the condition occurred due to labor, for if it did, there is no cure then, nor will it heal completely from that.
But in the case where it comes from a fall or a blow or something similar to these, the Ancients described the methods of treatment at great length, much of which I cannot recall; but I have abbreviated what little can excuse the abundance, due to my closeness to understanding and exposition, with which I have laid out and formed this from various instruments different from those with which I would show this and explain that.
And only those are cured which occur exactly as I describe on both sides.
And it is that the patient lies on his face near a wall, and lies below the bed in such a way that his feet are not touching the ground.
And nearby a straight post should be placed, planted in the ground at the end near his head, and another post around his feet at the other end of the bed.
And the minister should hold the post, and it should not be fixed in a hole; and another minister should hold the other according to that form.
And nearby wrap around the head of the patient and below the shoulders tie a strong bandage; and extend the end of the bandage to the post that is behind his head, and tie it there; and then stretch the other bandage over the hips of the patient with the hands behind the buttocks; and then adjust all the bandages and tie them to the other post that is behind his feet.
Then let the attendant extend the bandages at the part of the body, and do not remove the two limbs and the bandages from their places where they are placed, but rather do not secure them so firmly as we have said.
Then the physician should place his palms on the strong part of the spine until it returns, or he should place it on that table, and then adjust the patient's weight on the table until it returns.
But if it does not return by this method, now take a table whose width or length is about three arms, and dig into the wall near the patient a place where the end of the table can fit. Then place the middle of the table on the spine, and the physician should place his weight on the other end, and pull gently until the spine is compressed and returns to its proper place.
And if you wish, open the ring which is revolved around the hand; and it is that you place the patient on the ground with the head of the patient at the end of two poles, the length of which is one arm, and from the head of the patient to the other end, two similar poles; and the distance between each pole should be a palm, and they are fixed in the ground so that they do not move.
And there should be one round pole in which is the ring, and it should revolve around the two ends of the two poles.
And at the end of it is a knob in which the pole is formed, the length of which is one palm where it is revolved; and on the two other poles there is something similar to that.
Unbind the bandages that you have tightly bound to the patient’s feet at the time when you were about to cut his head, and the bandages that you have tightly bound around his thighs at the time when you were about to cut his legs.
You should have each turn held by a minister with his hand, and the surgeon should hold the spine as we have said.
And after the spine is returned and the place is adjusted, now you should apply an excipient poultice on white cloth, and then add wool; and then place over the poultice strips of wood whose width is three fingers or around that size, and whose length should cover the area affected by inflammation, and along the part of the spine.
And then bind it with the bandage as we have described, and let the patient use delicate nourishment until he is healed.
And if any inflammation remains in the area at the end of the healing process, now you should administer medicines that soften and soothe through prolonged application, as we have recommended; and a sheet of lead is also applied to that location.
And at times inflammation occurs in the back of the lower spine due to displacement; and that bone is one where it is not advisable to apply this medicine, as it could easily lead to fatal consequences.
Chapter XXXI.
On the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the bone and the joint of the muscle are subject only to dislocation, and do not suffer what happens to other joints with slight movement and concavity.
And the joint of the bone is dislocated in four ways, for it can be dislocated inwardly, outwardly, forward, or backward.
And it is most commonly dislocated inwardly, and rarely forward or backward.
And the sign of dislocation inward is that when you adjust the leg of the healthy side against the affected side, it is longer and the heel is more prominent than on the healthy side, and the patient cannot bend their knee towards their chest, and this location where bending occurs is often inflamed or swollen due to cautery, so the head of the bone has already reached here.
And the sign of one to whom dislocation occurs outwardly is that the symptoms of it are contrary to those mentioned earlier.
And the sign of one to whom dislocation occurs forward is that when lifting the leg according to its full range, it does not bend due to pain in the heel, and if they attempt to walk, they cannot step forward; and their urine is retained and their swallowing is swollen, and they must walk on the sole of their foot.
And the sign of one to whom dislocation occurs backward is that they cannot extend their heel, nor can they bend it forward as much as the other leg, and their leg is shorter than the other, and their throat is swollen, and the head of the bone is in that location.
The final reduction of the species of this dislocation is that you consider, at this time, if the dislocation is old and ancient, which already has been suffered by its owner for some time, you administer reduction to it and treat it according to its disposition; now it is not in that complete cure; and then there must be no expectation of full recovery.
In the case where the dislocation is recent and belongs to one of the four species of dislocation, now you must attend to this by returning the joint and extending it in both directions within and without, and move it to the right and left, for it easily returns and does not require any other treatment.
However, if it does not return in this manner, now you must prepare a strong assistant and extend the side of the limb from below, or along its sides, or use a bandage that is wrapped above the joint; and another assistant should extend the hand over there so as to place their hands on either side of the joint; then apply a lion's bandage tightly around the joint, and have another assistant hold the end of the bandage.
And this extension of the limb or of the part inside towards the clavicle, or of the part behind towards the back.
And perform the extension of all these in one motion while the patient is raised off the ground and remains hanging.
And this species of extension is common to all four types.
And so if the dislocation returns as we have said, it is good; and if not, now it is necessary for all species what I will say about its proper treatment.
In the reduction of the shoulder when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is towards the inner parts, it must be that the patient lies supine on his back; and then place the bandages along the ridge of the shoulder in the place where between the head of the shoulder and the place below the collarbone; and then extend the bandage from the angle as far as the outermost part of the clavicle.
And then another strong assistant should take hold of his arms and firmly grasp the thick part of the dislocated shoulder and extend it outward with great force, for that returns to its place.
And this particular type is the easiest to cure among those in which this member is reduced compared to the other types.
But if it slips away from you and does not obey in the exercise for this type of treatment at all, then bandages must be placed on both sides of the patient's body with a strong, light bandage over both heels and both toes.
And let the length of each be four fingers, and let the affected side be extended more than the other by a quantity of two fingers.
And then the patient should be suspended above the top of a post, and he should be in the hand; and let the distance from him to the ground be two arms' lengths.
And then command a strong youth to press down the head of the shoulder, and let another youth hold the patient, and the other youth pull the shoulder firmly upwards, for the joint returns to its place with speed.
But in the reduction of the shoulder when it is dislocated towards the outer part, it must be that the patient lies on his back as we have recommended in yellow fever; and the bandage should be tightly wrapped around the affected side properly and over the feet.
Place two poles beside the patient, one at the feet and the other at the head; and beside them place a pole adjusted to the middle of his back, placed upon which wet cloths are wrapped around him so that the patient is not harmed by the pole being among the things of him, so that the extension to the parts below is not pulled away.
And each attendant extends to his part, and the surgeon, with his hands, adjusts the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is to the front parts, then it is necessary that the bench be extended quickly, and according to this method described on the back; and the surgeon places the palm of his right hand on the patient's shoulder, and with the other hand he grasps it firmly, and with this firm grasp extends it to the rear parts toward the front.
And if the dislocation is to the rear parts, then it is not necessary that the patient be extended to the side parts and raised above the ground; rather, he should be placed on a hard surface, just as it is necessary for him similarly if his hip is dislocated outward, according to what we have said about the positioning of the body between two poles; and it is on his back with his limbs extended, as we have said, there is no harm.
And it is necessary to administer compression on the table at the location of the joint.
Thus, when the reduction is complete as it should be — and the sign of the reduction of the bone is not hidden from you, and that is when you extend the patient's two hands, and when you see those equal parts and the patient extends one hand and expands this one for an excuse, then you know that the limb as it should be has been returned — and then you adjust two things and pause in applying plaster and wrap both with a bandage so that the width is not diminished in any of the parts; and adhere the patient to rest for three days or four.
And then loosen the ligature and the plaster, and compare one hand with the other.
Now, if you see those equal parts clearly, then you know that the dislocation has already been fixed, and then let the patient go.
And if you see any sign of inflammation, then you return and plaster that area, and extend it according to your initial structure, and let it be for three days.
And then loosen that area, and allow the patient to delay in walking on each [affected part] for several days until it is comfortable.
Chapter XXXII.
On the Treatment of Dislocation of the Shoulder.
The shoulder can be dislocated in three ways: it can be dislocated to the outer parts, to the inner parts, and to the lower parts more so than to the rear; but it cannot be dislocated to the front parts.
And the sign of shoulder dislocation is that when you instruct the patient to apply their hand to the head, then you know that the shoulder is dislocated.
And for the restoration of all kinds of dislocations you should make the patient lie down straight; then place your hand on his chest if possible; press your two thumbs against his cheek, and put his foot between your hands; then firmly hold those hands over his ankle, and another assistant should extend his leg so that the bone returns to its proper place.
And the sign of successful reduction is that his chest adheres to the bed without laborious effort.
And then apply a plaster to that area and make the bandage adhere to the chest, and afterwards bind those areas together with a bandage for three days or four, and then loosen them.
And do not allow the patient to walk for several days until he is healed and comfortable.
But if the reduction of it to you is unnecessary, now administer strong extension with the instruments previously described in the treatment of the hip.
Chapter XXXIII.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the thigh.
The thigh sometimes is moved by slight displacement, and sometimes it is dislocated completely, and the dislocation of it occurs either towards the inside parts or towards the outside parts.
And the sign of the dislocation of it is that you see the thigh swollen towards the part to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment of its slight displacement is easy reduction, and it should be extended with ease by the hands until it returns to its place.
In the treatment of the shoulder when it is dislocated as a second step, make sure you have the patient lying straight, and let there be a strong and capable assistant behind him at his side.
And place yourself with your right hand above the uppermost part of his foot, and with your left hand below the lower part of his foot at the joint.
And pull the foot with your right hand, and then grasp it with your left hand for two moments, and then extend your left hand; and press the heel of the foot with your right hand vigorously.
And do this two times as we have counted.
And then press the heel of the foot with your hand for the third time, and you will be at the joint.
Then if it returns in one attempt or two according to this method, and if the foot feels equalized, it is good; and otherwise, immediately repeat the operation, for it will return.
But if you are prohibited from reducing it as we have recommended, then have the patient lie on his back on the ground, and place a strong board on firm ground under him, and make sure it fits between his shoulders, and you should wrap a cloth around him so that he does not slide.
And then the assistant hold his shoulders, and another assistant extend the foot with his hands or with straps placed on the back of the foot; and then each assistant pull against the extension on their side, and the board should be extended between his shoulders keeping him in place so that his body is not pulled towards the sides during the extension.
Then the surgeon pulls the dislocated parts apart, and another assistant holds the healthy limb above, for the dislocation returns with ease.
And so when the dislocation returns and the proper reduction is demonstrated to you, apply a plaster and wrap it tightly with bandages, and bind the limb securely to the bed.
And be careful to avoid the nerves which are located behind the heel, so that the bandage over them is not too tight and does not damage them severely.
Then leave it like that for two days or three; and if the bandage has loosened, wrap it again.
Then untie it on the third day or the fourth, and prohibit the patient from moving for forty days. But if the patient is forced to move before that time, there is no guarantee that the dislocation will not dissolve and be corrupted, and after that it may no longer be treatable.
And if an abscess occurs that requires cauterization, then you should proceed according to the instructions given in our narrative for the treatment and application described elsewhere, until it is healed.
Chapter XXXIV.
On the treatment of dislocation of the said feet.
Ensure that the person who is dislocated is pulled with some extension without excessive force; and what is not difficult is often easy.
But if the dislocation is in any of the joints of both feet, then you should have the patient lie down with their feet on a raised surface or table, and position them as if they were standing.
Here you should feel the pulse, and place your finger on what approaches of the joints, and then press that part with your finger firmly until it returns, and you see that it is inflamed and does not appear in a prominent location.
Then press the bottom of the foot against a board that fits the entire foot, which has two ends, and then stretch that part with proper firmness for three days; then loosen it and allow it to rest from walking for ten days, until it is stretched and its recovery is assured.
Chapter XXXV.
About the types of dislocation that are caused by plague or fracture or any such conditions.
When any cause of those conditions occurs, and you fail in the treatment of it and in the restoration of the patient, many times death follows.
And because of this, one should not take charge of the treatment unless they are wise in the art, experienced for a long time, merciful and pious, neither hasty nor presumptuous; and at the beginning use medicines that merely reduce swellings, and leave the patient to divine judgment, but refer them to someone from whom you hope for salvation from perdition, or relief from illness, where there appears any hope. And then strive to perform the reduction for the patient at the beginning of the cause, before it becomes a severe swelling.
And then if the member returns as we want it to, immediately administer a regimen that reduces severe swelling and treats the injury with what is appropriate from among the dried remedies.
And if the dislocation is with a fracture and it happens in the bones that are separated, then you perforate their membranes, and do what we have said about the simple diseases concerning what precedes in the places of those.
And guard and avoid according to your virtue the one of the instrument in the ways of fear as it has preceded in my preaching; for this is better for you to serve your Creator, and more permanent to your glory, and more salutary will be seen by you in your time. Thus ends the words of Albucasis perfectly; may God be blessed for them. Amen.
